  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_efa1e703-bc20-47ec-8299-a6dbaf95931f'
      });
    }

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1880235886" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1880235886" />
    <a title="Florencia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21575078-florencia"><img alt="Florencia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601866797p2/21575078.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1880235886?book_show_action=true">Jan 15, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Florencia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Florencia" href="/user/show/21575078-florencia">Florencia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21575078-florencia?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21575078-florencia?shelf=middle-east">middle-east</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21575078-florencia?shelf=philosophyland">philosophyland</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1880235886" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer529118867677053767"><blockquote>“Good morrow to thee, brother prisoner.”<br/>— Kahlil Gibran, "The Two Cages"</blockquote><br/>Another stop during this more diverse literary journey I decided to embark on this year. I chose the highly acclaimed prose of Kahlil Gibran, a man to whose land I'm connected through blood - half Lebanese, half Italian; nothing to do with my innocuous obsession with Russian and Japanese literature, but well, who can control those things anyway?<br/><br/>Before I immerse myself in the depths of the universe Gibran created in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2547.The_Prophet' title='The Prophet by Kahlil Gibran' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Proph</a></span>
  <span id="freeText529118867677053767" style="display:none"><blockquote>“Good morrow to thee, brother prisoner.”<br />— Kahlil Gibran, "The Two Cages"</blockquote><br />Another stop during this more diverse literary journey I decided to embark on this year. I chose the highly acclaimed prose of Kahlil Gibran, a man to whose land I'm connected through blood - half Lebanese, half Italian; nothing to do with my innocuous obsession with Russian and Japanese literature, but well, who can control those things anyway?<br /><br />Before I immerse myself in the depths of the universe Gibran created in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2547.The_Prophet" title="The Prophet by Kahlil Gibran" rel="nofollow noopener">The Prophet</a>, I decided to get acquainted with his writing and views by reading another book not as widely known. I chose <i>The Madman</i> because I found it somewhat amusing that it wasn't the first time I read a madman's words:<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18778193.Memories_of_a_Madman_and_November" title="Memories of a Madman and November by Gustave Flaubert" rel="nofollow noopener">Memories of a Madman and November</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11904357.Diary_of_a_Madman" title="Diary of a Madman by Nikolai Gogol" rel="nofollow noopener">Diary of a Madman</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17879653.The_Diary_of_a_Madman" title="The Diary of a Madman by Guy de Maupassant" rel="nofollow noopener">The Diary of a Madman</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/21442812.Diary_of_a_Madman" title="Diary of a Madman by Lu Xun" rel="nofollow noopener">Diary of a Madman</a> (to read soon)<br /><br />I won't expand on the cliché of a madman's words being more truthful and reasonable than the speech of any other human being considered <i>sane</i> by ordinary standards. I will just say that this collection includes a variety of profound and intriguing parables that constitute a faithful portrait of humanity. The following is one of my favorites.<br /><br /><blockquote><b>The Seven Selves</b><br />In the stillest hour of the night, as I lay half asleep, my seven selves sat together and thus conversed in whisper: <br /><br />First Self: Here, in this madman, I have dwelt all these years, with naught to do but renew his pain by day and recreate his sorrow by night. I can bear my fate no longer, and now I rebel. <br /><br />Second Self: Yours is a better lot than mine, brother, for it is given to me to be this madman’s joyous self. I laugh his laughter and sing his happy hours, and with thrice winged feet I dance his brighter thoughts. It is I that would rebel against my weary existence. <br /><br />Third Self: And what of me, the love-ridden self, the flaming brand of wild passion and fantastic desires? It is I the love-sick self who would rebel against this madman. <br /><br />Fourth Self: I, amongst you all, am the most miserable, for naught was given me but odious hatred and destructive loathing. It is I, the tempest-like self, the one born in the black caves of Hell, who would protest against serving this madman. <br /><br />Fifth Self: Nay, it is I, the thinking self, the fanciful self, the self of hunger and thirst, the one doomed to wander without rest in search of unknown things and things not yet created; it is I, not you, who would rebel. <br /><br />Sixth Self: And I, the working self, the pitiful labourer, who, with patient hands, and longing eyes, fashion the days into images and give the formless elements new and eternal forms—it is I, the solitary one, who would rebel against this restless madman. <br /><br />Seventh Self: How strange that you all would rebel against this man, because each and every one of you has a preordained fate to fulfil. Ah! could I but be like one of you, a self with a determined lot! But I have none, I am the do-nothing self, the one who sits in the dumb, empty nowhere and nowhen, while you are busy re-creating life. Is it you or I, neighbours, who should rebel? <br /><br />When the seventh self thus spake the other six selves looked with pity upon him but said nothing more; and as the night grew deeper one after the other went to sleep enfolded with a new and happy submission. <br /><br />But the seventh self remained watching and gazing at nothingness, which is behind all things.</blockquote><br /><br /><br /><br />Jan 29, 17<br />*** Also on <a href="https://nonbookreviews.wordpress.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">my blog</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="529118867677053767" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3015800977658389418">The Madman, Kahlil Gibran <br/>Khalil Gibran, also known as Kahlil Gibran, was born on January 6, 1883 in Northern Lebanon. As a result of his family's poverty, he received no formal education as a small child but had regular visits from the local priest who taught him about the Bible as well as the Syrian and Arabic languages. After his father was imprisoned for embezzlement and his family's property was confiscated by the authorities, his mother decided to emigrate to the United States in 1895. The</span>
  <span id="freeText3015800977658389418" style="display:none">The Madman, Kahlil Gibran <br />Khalil Gibran, also known as Kahlil Gibran, was born on January 6, 1883 in Northern Lebanon. As a result of his family's poverty, he received no formal education as a small child but had regular visits from the local priest who taught him about the Bible as well as the Syrian and Arabic languages. After his father was imprisoned for embezzlement and his family's property was confiscated by the authorities, his mother decided to emigrate to the United States in 1895. They settled in Boston's South End. He attended public school and art school, where he was introduced to the artist, photographer, and publisher Fred Holland Day. A publisher used some of Gibran's drawings for book covers in 1898. His family forced him to return to Lebanon to complete his education and learn the Arabic language. He enrolled in Madrasat-al-Hikmah, a Masonite-founded school, which offered a nationalistic curriculum partial to church writings, history and liturgy. He learned Arabic, French, and excelled in poetry. He returned to the United States in 1902. In 1904, he hosted his first art exhibit, which featured his allegorical and symbolic charcoal drawings. During this exhibition, he met Mary Elizabeth Haskell, who would go on to fund Gibran's artistic development for nearly his entire life. Not only was he an artist, but he also wrote poetry and other works including The Madman, The Prophet, and Sand and Foam. He died of cirrhosis of the liver and tuberculosis on April 10, 1931.<br />عنوانها: المجنون، دیوانه؛ سرگشته؛ حیران؛ نویسنده: خلیل جبران؛ تاریخ نخستین خوانش: بیست و سوم ماه جولای سال 2009 میلادی<br />عنوان: سرگشته؛ خلیل جبران، مترجم: سیمین پناهی، تهران، نیک فرجام، 1386ريال در 104 ص؛<br />با داستانهای دیگر ایشان بسیار چاپ شده است<br />ا. شربیانی</span>

  <span id="freeText12176837843972220785" style="display:none">"You ask me how I became a madman. <span id="freeTextContainer16926274104003004413">A couple of slim volumes by Kahlil Gibran has been lying around in my house since I can remember. I had a try at reading them during my teens, but could not enter into his strange world. Now, after a gap of thirty five years, I realise why I couldn't empathise with him at the time.<br/><br/>Gibran's world is romantic, but shot through with a streak of melancholy. His work - you may call it prose poetry, fable, aphorism or just admit it defies definition - has to be experienced, not analysed. And it has go</span>
  <span id="freeText16926274104003004413" style="display:none">A couple of slim volumes by Kahlil Gibran has been lying around in my house since I can remember. I had a try at reading them during my teens, but could not enter into his strange world. Now, after a gap of thirty five years, I realise why I couldn't empathise with him at the time.<br /><br />Gibran's world is romantic, but shot through with a streak of melancholy. His work - you may call it prose poetry, fable, aphorism or just admit it defies definition - has to be experienced, not analysed. And it has got a certain quality of timelessness. I posted a piece on FB and someone told me it was so appropriate for today!<br /><br />Sit back in your chair with the drink of your choice, open this book at random, pick out a gem and savour it slowly. Perfect way to while away an evening. </span>

<div id="review_1056605174" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1056605174" />
    <a title="S.Ach" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/233507-s-ach"><img alt="S.Ach" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515914630p2/233507.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1056605174?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="S.Ach" class="user" itemprop="url" name="S.Ach" href="/user/show/233507-s-ach">S.Ach</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780486419114" href="/book/show/820151.The_Madman">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/233507-s-ach?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1056605174" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13213774301762495038">Kahlil Gibran's words need to be felt, not analyzed. <br/>Here is a snippet...<br/><br/><b>When my sorrow was born</b><br/><br/>When my sorrow was born I nursed it with care, and watched over it with loving tenderness.<br/><br/>And my Sorrow grew like all living things, strong and beautiful and full of wondrous delights.<br/><br/>And we loved one another, my Sorrow and I, and we loved the world about us; for Sorrow had a kindly heart and mine was kindly with Sorrow.<br/><br/>And when we conversed, my Sorrow and I, our days were winged and our nights were g</span>
  <span id="freeText13213774301762495038" style="display:none">Kahlil Gibran's words need to be felt, not analyzed. <br />Here is a snippet...<br /><br /><b>When my sorrow was born</b><br /><br />When my sorrow was born I nursed it with care, and watched over it with loving tenderness.<br /><br />And my Sorrow grew like all living things, strong and beautiful and full of wondrous delights.<br /><br />And we loved one another, my Sorrow and I, and we loved the world about us; for Sorrow had a kindly heart and mine was kindly with Sorrow.<br /><br />And when we conversed, my Sorrow and I, our days were winged and our nights were girdled with dreams; for Sorrow had an eloquent tongue, and mine was eloquent with Sorrow.<br /><br />And when we sang together, my Sorrow and I, our neighbors sat at their windows and listenend; for our songs were deep as the sea and our melodies were full of strange memories.<br /><br />And when we walked together, my Sorrow and I, people gazed at us with gentle eyes and whispered in words of exceeding sweetness. And there were those who looked with envy upon us, for Sorrow was a noble thing and I was proud with Sorrow.<br /><br />But my Sorrow died, like all living things, and alone I am left to muse and ponder.<br /><br />And now when I speak my words fall heavily upon my ears.<br />And when I sing my songs my neighbours come not to listen.<br />And when I walk the streets no one looks at me.<br /><br />Only in my sleep I hear voices saying in pity, "See, there lies the man whose Sorrow is dead."<br /><br /><br /><b>And when my joy was born</b><br /><br />And when my joy was born I held it in my arms and stood on the house-top shouting, "Come ye, my neighbours, come and see, for Joy this day is born unto me. Come and behold this gladsome thing that laugheth in the sun."<br /><br />But none of my neighbours came to look upon my Joy, and great was my astonishment.<br />And every day for seven moons I proclaimed my Joy from the house-top -- and yet no one heeded me. And my Joy and I were alone, unsought and unvisited.<br /><br />Then my Joy grew pale and weary because no other heart but mine held its loveliness and no other lips kissed its lips.<br /><br />Then my Joy died of isolation.<br /><br />And now I only remember my dead Joy in remembering my dead Sorrow. But memory is an autumn leaf that murmurs in the wind and then is heard no more.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13213774301762495038" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText8903007794069146853" style="display:none">Thus I became a madman.<br />And I have found both freedom and safety in my madness; the freedom of loneliness and the safety from being understood, for those who understand us enslave something in us.<br /><br />هكذا صرت مجنوناً<br />ولكني وجدت بجنوني هذا, الحرية والنجاة معاً<br />حرية الإنفراد, والنجاة من أن يدرك الناس كياني<br />.لأن الذين يُدركون كياننا إنما يستعبدون بعض ما فينا<br /><br />It is beautifully inspiring!<br /><br />It took me one hour to swim deeply in Gibran's wide ocean of ideas! <br />Literary speaking from a fanciful point of view, One has to read this masterpiece by the breathing of the heart. Gibran's idyllic feelings lead me to quench my literay,artistically and philosophically thirst by his outstanding fountain of wisdom. <br />The Madman is a book full of joy,'queer' thoughts, vivid brightness and love of wisdom.It is a light that can enlighten a reader's inner path.<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_717694732" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/717694732" />
    <a title="Momina Masood" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17262906-momina-masood"><img alt="Momina Masood" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1423900868p2/17262906.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/717694732?book_show_action=true">Sep 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Momina Masood" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Momina Masood" href="/user/show/17262906-momina-masood">Momina Masood</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Everyone!</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17262906-momina-masood?shelf=pop-philosophy">pop-philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer717694732" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1378642548759884643">It was <i>My Friend</i> that did it, that kindled a fire within my heart and now that I have finished this book I am still strangely brimming with emotion, though more calm than before. You read some books, some words, a passage even, and wonder what on earth were you doing before! Why were you wasting your time with nonsense when something like this was present and could have been read instead! This is truly a very, very beautiful book and I can only imagine how his fuller books like <i>The Prophet</i> would</span>
  <span id="freeText1378642548759884643" style="display:none">It was <i>My Friend</i> that did it, that kindled a fire within my heart and now that I have finished this book I am still strangely brimming with emotion, though more calm than before. You read some books, some words, a passage even, and wonder what on earth were you doing before! Why were you wasting your time with nonsense when something like this was present and could have been read instead! This is truly a very, very beautiful book and I can only imagine how his fuller books like <i>The Prophet</i> would be like. <i>War</i> was a small rendition of a childhood story my father used to tell my brother and I and it was one of our favorites. <i>The Wise King</i> is somewhere told in one of Paulo Coelho's books, <i>The Devil and Miss Prym</i> maybe, though I'm not sure. It always feels sweet reading something familiar. Amid these sweet, familiar somethings are also sour, little ironies and taunts, most prominent in <i>The Scarecrow</i>, <i>The Fox</i>, and <i>The Sleep-Walkers</i>. <i>The Pomegranate</i> made me laugh, and I liked the sound of that laugh, which is something! <i>The Grave-Digger</i> has mystical colors, <i>The Two Learned Men</i> quietly puts you in your place and advises you in hushed tones, <i>Crucified</i> is poetry, beautiful, powerful poetry, <i>The Greater Sea</i> is honest and truthful and thought-provoking, <i>Faces</i> reminded me that to get hold of reality, we must first learn to look through and beyond our own reflection and projection. It is strange but true that if you try to look hard into someone's eyes, you see your own reflection there. This is where Gibran says, "... I look through the fabric my own eye weaves, and behold the reality beneath." Only when one learns to look through one's own reflection, prejudices and preconceptions, and beyond them can then one hope to attain a glimpse of truth. And what a big, long paragraph!<br /><br />The language and poetry of <i>Night and the Madman</i> will swallow you in and throw you out intoxicated, breathless, subdued. I have yet to gather my thoughts on <i>The Blessed City</i>, and make my peace with it. <i>The Three Ants</i> again advises you like <i>The Two Learned Men</i>, and it's advice worth taking. Well, there you have it. Read, in God's name, read Gibran if you haven't already! He will make you fall in love with him in two pages and show you a mirror in which you'll see yourself somewhere. I, at least, saw myself and heard myself and was terrified and pleased, at the same time. Time enough, readers, to read something that could do that to you. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1378642548759884643" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

But now the nagging suspicion of arti-fartiness stinks up my perceptions, although given the small length and public domain availability, you</span>

But now the nagging suspicion of arti-fartiness stinks up my perceptions, although given the small length and public domain availability, you'd be mad not to read this book.</span>

<div id="review_1709435153" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1709435153" />
    <a title="Shahad takleef" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35406752-shahad-takleef"><img alt="Shahad takleef" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513107527p2/35406752.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1709435153?book_show_action=true">Jul 26, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shahad takleef" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shahad takleef" href="/user/show/35406752-shahad-takleef">Shahad takleef</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1709435153" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17608411736893409435"><blockquote>
  <b>thus i became a madman <br/>And I have found both freedom of loneliness and the safety from being understood, for those who understand us enslave something in us. </b>
</blockquote><br/><br/>this book is one of these too dear to my heart , this is another of Gibran books that is labeled as one of my all time favorites , i was writing the quote above and the Arabic form of <i>"for those who understand us enslave something in us" </i> "لأن الذين يُدركون كياننا إنما يستعبدون بعض ما فينا" was playing in my head like an old mantra from</span>
  <span id="freeText17608411736893409435" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <b>thus i became a madman <br />And I have found both freedom of loneliness and the safety from being understood, for those who understand us enslave something in us. </b>
</blockquote><br /><br />this book is one of these too dear to my heart , this is another of Gibran books that is labeled as one of my all time favorites , i was writing the quote above and the Arabic form of <i>"for those who understand us enslave something in us" </i> "لأن الذين يُدركون كياننا إنما يستعبدون بعض ما فينا" was playing in my head like an old mantra from years ago , its one of these quotes that shock you when you first read them , then they are unforgettable , a frozen fraction of time , they are suddenly in your mind pockets till forever .<br />this book is a collection of short parables written with a sardonic tone which is both introspective and easily accessible . its fascinating in how wealthy the book is with meaning that's just hidden beneath the text . take this one as an example : <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <b><br />Once I said to a scarecrow, “You must be tired of standing in this lonely field.” <br />And he said, “The joy of scaring is a deep and lasting one, and I never tire of it.” <br />Said I, after a minute of thought, “It is true; for I too have known that joy.” <br />Said he, “Only those who are stuffed with straw can know it.” <br />Then I left him, not knowing whether he had complimented or belittled me. <br />A year passed, during which the scarecrow turned philosopher. <br />And when I passed by him again I saw two crows building a nest under .</b>
</blockquote><br /><br />i remember the first time i read this , i didnt get it . i had to re-read it several times to get the hidden meaning and Uncovering it was like figuring out a puzzle . which its solution , if you are curious , is that the scarecrow was feeling power and pride in being something to be feared , but then only those whom inside is stuffed with straw no more would feel this way , and when wisdom filled him years after he figured out that this empty isolation and resentment are the misrepresentation of things . so he made friends with those who he used to scare away , he found company in a lonely field ..<br />and what a beautiful message it is to be delivered in few word in a simple story as that .<br /><br />through out the book there's a subject he repeats under several titles , the different perspectives of people and putting one's self in the others shoes , we can see that in <b>The eye , Said a blade of grass </b> and <b>The three ants .</b><br />all in all this one book , its words are not to be analysed but rather to be deeply felt in its communication and its inner loneliness .</span>
  <a data-text-id="17608411736893409435" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213058-sebastian-church?shelf=goodreads-2">goodreads-2</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer119022949" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7477063200833110416">This book taught me a great deal, not only about life, but about poetry as well. It gave me a completely knew perspective on how poetry can be written and structured, that poetry is something that isn't bound by a particular style or shape. The short parables throughout the book fascinated me in that their brevity meant a wealthy of meaning was hidden just beneath the text. Uncovering the meanings of these parables was like figuring out a book full of puzzles, and this was perhaps the most enter</span>
  <span id="freeText7477063200833110416" style="display:none">This book taught me a great deal, not only about life, but about poetry as well. It gave me a completely knew perspective on how poetry can be written and structured, that poetry is something that isn't bound by a particular style or shape. The short parables throughout the book fascinated me in that their brevity meant a wealthy of meaning was hidden just beneath the text. Uncovering the meanings of these parables was like figuring out a book full of puzzles, and this was perhaps the most entertaining, original, unprecedented experience I have had in reading. To call Gibran wise is a slight, because even the wisdom a wise man tries to communicate always sounds foolish, as the adage goes. Gibran is beyond wise because he has been able to capture wisdom in verses and stanzas that call out to us from our inner being. The Madman definitely taught me much about poetry and opened a universe of possibility for me to explore and play in with my writing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7477063200833110416" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText18037323614437092339" style="display:none">the most mesmerizing part is that about sorrow; When my Sorrow was born I nursed it with care, and watched over it with loving tenderness. <br /><br />And my Sorrow grew like all living things, strong and beautiful and full of wondrous delights. <br /><br />And we loved one another, my Sorrow and I, and we loved the world about us; for Sorrow had a kindly heart and mine was kindly with Sorrow. <br /><br />And when we conversed, my Sorrow and I, our days were winged and our nights were girdled with dreams; for Sorrow had an eloquent tongue, and mine was eloquent with Sorrow. <br /><br />And when we sang together, my Sorrow and I, our neighbours sat at their windows and listened; for our songs were deep as the sea and our melodies were full of strange memories. <br /><br />And when we walked together, my Sorrow and I, people gazed at us with gentle eyes and whispered in words of exceeding sweetness. And there were those who looked with envy upon us, for Sorrow was a noble thing and I was proud with Sorrow. <br /><br />But my Sorrow died, like all living things, and alone I am left to muse and ponder. <br /><br />And now when I speak my words fall heavily upon my ears. <br /><br />And when I sing my songs my neighbours come not to listen. <br /><br />And when I walk the streets no one looks at me. <br /><br />Only in my sleep I hear voices saying in pity, “See, there lies the man whose Sorrow is dead.”</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('TMrDxvgk513APgj7OJrioYQPoEmEP1EI9Gly30TtosK8VPjEOjGHeCtaooG+YaE/B597TDpEgiZhwAyGflMSfQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

Önce tiyatro yapıtları ve opera librettoları yazdı. 1863’te Le Magasin d’Education et de Récréation’da, Voyages extraordinaire (Olağanüstü Yolculuklar) adlı dizinin ilk yapıtı olarak yayımladığı Cinq semaines en ballon (1863; Balonla Beş Hafta) büyük ilgi gördü. Bunun üzerine fantastik serüvenler yazmaya devam etti. Voyage au centre de la Terre (1864; Dünyanın Merkezine Yolculuk), Vingt mille lieues sous les mers (1870; Deniz Altında Yirmi Bin Fersah) ve L’Île mystérieuse (1874; Esrarlı Ada) bunlardan bazılarıdır. Schopenhauer’e göre yaratıkların en mutsuzu insandır ve her türlü oluşun kaynağı olan irade, dünyadaki bütün kötülüklerin çıkış noktasıdır. Dünya kötü bir yerdir ve kötülüğünün en belirleyici yönü, acı ve mutsuzluğun her yerde hazır ve nazır oluşudur.\n\nMutlu Olma Sanatı, iyimser dünya görüşüne karşı çıkan ve yaşadığımız dünyayı olası dünyaların en kötüsü sayan Schopenhauer’den umulmadık bir kılavuz.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/6466154.Kahlil_Gibran">About Kahlil Gibran</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/6466154.Kahlil_Gibran"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1353732571p3/6466154.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/6466154.Kahlil_Gibran">Kahlil Gibran
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
12,439
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17289909241885653669">Kahlil Gibran (Arabic: <b>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/17888244.________________' title='جبران خليل جبران' rel='nofollow noopener'>جبران خليل جبران</a>
</b>) was a Lebanese-American artist, poet, and writer. <br/>Born in the town of Bsharri in modern-day Lebanon (then part of Ottoman Mount Lebanon), as a young man he emigrated with his family to the United States where he studied art and began his literary career. In the Arab world, Gibran is regarded as a literary and political rebel. His romantic style was at t</span>
  <span id="freeText17289909241885653669" style="display:none">Kahlil Gibran (Arabic: <b>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/17888244.________________" title="جبران خليل جبران" rel="nofollow noopener">جبران خليل جبران</a>
</b>) was a Lebanese-American artist, poet, and writer. <br />Born in the town of Bsharri in modern-day Lebanon (then part of Ottoman Mount Lebanon), as a young man he emigrated with his family to the United States where he studied art and began his literary career. In the Arab world, Gibran is regarded as a literary and political rebel. His romantic style was at the heart of a renaissance in modern Arabic literature, especially prose poetry, breaking away from the classical school. In Lebanon, he is still celebrated as a literary hero. <br />He is chiefly known in the English-speaking world for his 1923 book <i>The Prophet</i>, an early example of inspirational fiction including a series of philosophical essays written in poetic English prose. The book sold well despite a cool critical reception, gaining popularity in the 1930s and again especially in the 1960s counterculture. <br />Gibran is the third best-selling poet of all time, behind Shakespeare and Lao-Tzu.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17289909241885653669" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/6517-the-madman">Quotes from The Madman</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
